4.26       The detailed disbursement schedule for the project is given in
Annex 4-10. It differs from the standard West Africa Region profile for
Transport Sector Projects of 6.5 years mainly because preparation of the
road and port components is well advanced, so that rapid project
start-up can be expected.
4.27       Road Component.  The standard disbursement profile for trans-
port projects in the West African Region indicates that a six-year
period  is required  for  full  disbursement.   But  the profile  reflects
mainly the experience of complex highway maintenance projects and is not
considered  appropriate  for  the  proposed project.   About  94%  of  the
proposed credit concern straightforward civil works, for which the bulk
of final design data already exist and that are ready for bidding by
already prequalified firms. Therefore, the proposed highway component
is expected to be fully disbursed by end 1990. The detailed disburse-
ment schedule is shown in Annex 4-11. The table above indicates credit
categories and disbursement percentages. Disbursemnent for civil works



- 24 -
and consulting services would be on the basis of contracts approved by
IDA; it is estimated that a maximum of about 10 contracts would be
signed.  Disbursements for purchase of equipment and furniture would
also be made on the basis of IDA-approved contracts, and equipment for
the vegetation control operation would be acquired through the OPR
purchasing agent.
4.28      Port Component.  The IDA financing of US$11.0 m equivalent for
this component will be onlent to PAC at commercial rates (8.00% for a
period of twenty years, including a four-year grace period) to cover 20%
of the foreign exchange  cost of the component.   The remaining  80%
(US$44 m equivalent) is expected to be financed by an AfDB loan of
US$30.0 m equivalent (8.64%, also for twentv years, including a
four-year grace period), and by a KfW/SJF credit of DM 25 m (US$13.0 m
equivalent, 8%, same repayment and grace periods) and a GTZ grant of
US$1.0 m equivalent. PAC will provide US$5.0 m equivalent representing
the local expenditures.
4.29      AfDB will finance the container berth, dredging and construc-
tion supervision; KfW will finance the siltation prevention dike,
extension of the Prudente dike and preparation of tender documents; GTZ
will finance expatriate experts providing the technical assistance; and
IDA will finance the fuel jetty, rehabilitation works and navigation
aids, and training and studies.  Effectiveness of the AfDB loan and of
the KfW/SJF credit will be conditions of disbursement for the port
component of the credit.  The detailed disbursement schedule and the
agreed financing plan are shown in Annex 4-12.
4.30      Aviation Component.  The aviation component will finance 100%
of foreign cost estimated at US$12.2 m. US$11 m will be financed by IDA
and US$1.2 m equivalent from a grant provided by FAC (French bilateral
assistance); the latter would cover technical assistance. Local costs
will be financed by Government as a US$0.8 m equity contribution to the
Air Navigation Agency in annual installments as required for the imple-
mentation of the project.  This was agreed at negotiations.   The IDA
credit would finance 100% of the foreign cost of studies, training,
civil works, purchase and installation of equipment (plus possibly a
minor portion of technical assistance if the FAC grant is not sufficienat
to cover its full cost).  Except for the part financing the equipment
for METEOROLOGIE NATIONALE, the IDA credit will be onlent on commercial
terms to the ANA; this was confirmed at negotiations. Disbursament for
all equipment and works would only start after key members of the
technical assistance team have taken up their duty and prepared the
specifications and tender documents.
4.31      Transport Sector Component.  The IDA credit will finance 100%
of the technical assistance, consulting services and training costs
under this component. Detailed disbursement schedules for the aviation
and transport sector components are in Annex 4-13.
G. Accounting and Auditing
4.32      Accounting, costing, and budgeting systems for the road and
aviation components need to be established by the Project Management
Unit in SETP and technical assistance to SET/ANA.  They have already



- 25 -
been established in PAC, and will be further improved through special
financial assistance and introduction of computerized management sys-
tems.  External auditors acceptable to the Association have carried out
annual audits of PAC and will continue to do so; similar arrangements
will be made for SETP and SET/ANA, with audit reports to be submitted to
IDA within four months of the end of each fiscal year.
H. Reporting
4.33      SETP, SET/ANA and PAC, with the assistance of consultants,
will submit quarterly progress reports on project implementation and
expenditures to IDA and the co-financiers, as appropriate.
I. Impact on Environment and Employment
4.34      The  rehabilitation  of  existing paved  roads  will  have  no
negative  environmental  impact.   The pilot maintenance operation for
vegetation control may have a positive impact by reducing the number of
bush fires.  The road component will generate employment for about
1,500 unskilled laborers during construction. If the pilot maintenance
operation for vegetation control is successful, additional permanent
jobs would be created through private small contractors doing routine
maintenance by contract.
4.35      No significant environmental impact is expected in the city of
Conakry or adjacent water areas as a result of the port component since
all improvements are confined either to rehabilitation of existing
facilities that have operated in the same location for many years or to
construction of berths within the same port water area. Hydraulic model
studies proved that closing the two openings located at the north side
of the harbor will have no adverse scouring or siltation effect on the
coast adjacent to Conakry. Dredged soil will be dumped in deep sea far
from the port in a place which will have no adverse effect on either the
navigation or the environment.
4.36      The port  component  will  generate  employment  opportunities
during the construction stage for some 400 skilled and unskilled labor.
On completion of the project, about 100 permanent additional jobs wouild
be created, ranging from port labor to skilled and semi-skilled workers
in the operation and maintenance of the facilities to be provided under
the project. Training will improve skills in port management, security
and accounting of about 50 PAC staff.
4.37      While there will be inter-agency transfers under the aviation
component, little or no changes are expected in employment levels.
J. Technical Assistance
4.38      The staff-month requirements for technical assistance exclud-
ing construction supervision total 553.  They include 90 s-m for the
road component, 223 s-m for the port component, 120 s-m for the aviation
component and 120 s-m for the transport sector.



- 26 -
V. ECONOMIC EVALUATION
A. The Road Component
General
5.01      The project complements and completes a program of paved road
rehabilitation launched under the ongoing Third Highway Project and
initiates the reorganization of the Public Works Administration.  The
economic evaluation of the civil works to be carried out under the
project is based on the Highway Design and Maintenance Standards Model
(HDMS).   Vehicle operating costs  (voc) have been computed based on
roughness indices, using the vehicle operating cost module of the
HDM III model, and reflecting the present composition of the vehicle
fleet in Guinea described in Annex 5-1. All costs and benefits in the
economic analysis are estimated at January 1987 prices, net of taxes and
duties.
Economic Rates of Return
5.02      In calculating the economic rates of return  (ERR) for the
three road sections, it was conservatively assumed that (a) maintenance
costs would increase compared to the "without project" situation, where
no maintenance is actually carried out; (b) roads would be resealed in
year 10; (c) traffic would increase by only 4% annually, and there would
be no generated traffic; and (d) passenger time savings were disregard-
ed. Also, any residual value of the roads in year 15 was ignored. With
these very conservative assumptions, the estimated ERRs are as shown
below:
A                   B                  C
Mamou-Kissidougou    Kissidougou-Gueck6dou    Kamsar-Bok
Length (km)                  208                  93                 63
ADT (veh/day)                187                  142                165
Investment (USs m)           15.4                 6.3                0.8
ERR (%)                      22                   18                 28
5.03      Sensitivity tests show these results to be quite robust:  for
the ERR to drop to 12%, average daily traffic on road A would have to
decrease to 109 vehicles reducing VOC savings by one-third, or alterna-
tively costs would have to increase by 80%; on road B, adt would have to
drop to 103 vehicles reducing benefits by 22%, or costs would have to
increase by 40%; while resealing of road C would still be justified with
adt of 58 vehicles only, and a 12% ERR would still be assured if the
cost of investments rose by 280%. Details of the economic analysis are
in Annex 5-2.



- 27 -
Project Beneficiaries
5.04      By reestablishing normal traffic conditions on essential paved
road sections, the project will enable agricultural inputs and basic
commodities to reach the regions, and agricultural surplus production to
be exported to the markets of Conakry. It will thus benefit both rural
and urban populations by facilitating inter-regional exchanges of goods
and reestablishing safe all-weather passenger transport at substantially
shorter travel times.  Most directly, the project will benefit road
users, including vehicle owners and paying passengers who should benefit
from tariff reductions due to significantly reduced costs of vehicle
operation and enhanced competitiveness of the road transport industry.
Project Risks
5.05      Provided that IDA can assure intensive supervision at crucial
periods of contract preparation, award and start-up of works to help
unblock current cumbersome project implementation procedures in Guinea,
there should be no particular risks attached to the timely execution of
this straightforward and urgent civil works component.
B. The Port Component
General
5.06      The proposed project continues IDA's earlier assistance to
Guinea's port sub-sector in (a) developing containerized traffic through
the construction of a new deep-water container terminal; (b) re-estab-
lishing and improving earlier mineral (bauxite-alumina) export capacity
by siltation prevention and establishment dredging of the port water
area and access channel to -9.5 m:n and (c) reducing ore carrier waiting
time and substantially improve port safety through construction of a
specialized fuel jetty.  At the same time, the project would support
institutional improvements in the port sub-sector.
5.07      The economic objectives of the project are to:  (a) signifi-
cantly reduce existing and expected future ship-waiting time, and
related costs and congestion surcharges;  (b) avoid expensive overland
transport costs, and port revenues foregone on future traffic which
would otherwise be diverted  to neighboring Freetown port in Sierra
Leone; (c) substantially reduce maintenance dredging costs through
siltation prevention; (d) eliminate losses on mineral exports foregone
by re-establishing the requisite depth in the port area and access
channel for ore-carriers to operate fully loaded; (e) improve operation-
al efficiency and navigational safety in the entrance channel and port
area through dredging and renewal of navigation aids; and (f) increase
port revenues by strengthening and improving port operation and adminis-
tration.  Further, the economic analysis of the proposed port project
component excludes the improvements in port productivity expected from
the first Port Project, which have been considered as already attained
in the "without" case.



- 28 -
Traffic Analysis and Projections
5.08      Consultants prepared detailed traffic forecasts, on a commodi-
ty basis through the year 2000, giving a moderate and a pessimistic
scenario, which differ mainly in the volume of mineral exports and
mining-related imports.  The pessimistic scenario implies an average
annual growth rate for imports during the next ten years of 5.3% (made
up of a 6% growth in general merchandise and a 4% growth in bulk im-
ports) and no growth in exports (agricultural export growth of 8% p.a.
being insignificant in the volume of overall exports of around
3,300,000 tpy). While this is well below the untypical growth rates of
recent years, it was considered prudent to further reduce growth assump-
tions for imports for 1987-95 to 4% and to 3.5% from 1996-2000 reflect-
ing lower imports mainly of rice, cement and "other merchandise," in
line with local production objectives and foreign exchange constraints.
Traffic forecasts used in the economic analysis are detailed in An-
nex 2-6, 2 and in Annex 6-3, Table 1.
Project Benefits
5.09      Project benefits were calculated for the port component as a
whole, as well as for each of the three main sub-components: (a) silta-
tion prevention dike, establishment dredging and rehabilitation of
navigation aids; (b) the deepwater container terminal and minor rehabil-
itation works; and (c) the petroleum jetty. The quantified benefits for
the project include: for (a) above -- (i) reduced maintenance dredging
costs; (ii) reduced shipwaiting time attributable to dredging before the
quays; and (iii) reduced transport cost per ton of mineral shipped due
to full ship loading; for (b) above -- (i) avoided shipwaiting time due
to increased port capacity and expedited berthing; and avoided overland
transport costs for goods which would otherwise have to be diverted to
Freetown; and (ii) reduction in losses due to improved security in a
separate, privately managed container terminal; and for (c) above --
avoided shipwaiting costs at the mineral quays currently blocked for
significant periods when fuel is discharged and avoided costs of reha-
bilitating the pipeline from the FRIGUIA quai to the oil depot.
5.10      Benefits which have not been quantified, but which have been
important considerations in designing the project include increased
navigational safety due to dredging and signalization (one ore-carrier
ran aground in the access channel in December 1986 and blocked port
access for several days); better infrastructure utilization due to the
possibility of using the access channel during night-time; reduced
inventory capital for goods tied up in waiting ships; avoidance of major
fire risks at the non-adapted mineral qaays where fuel is currently
discharged; and finally, the general benefits from increased productivi-
ty in port operations and cargo handling due to training assistance and
private sector involvement in port-associated services.
Economic Rate of Return (ERR) and Sensitivity Analysis
5.11      Based on  the conservative  estimate of quantified benefits
detailed in para 5.09, estimated capital and maintenance/operating cost,
and a 20-year economic life, the project would yield an ERR of 24%
overall; with siltation prevention/dredging/navigation aids contributing
22%; the container terminal 27% and the fuel jetty 12%. An increase in



- 29 -
project costs of 20% would reduce the ERR to 21%; a similar decrease in
benefits would result in an ERR of 20%.  Even with a simultaneous 20%
increase in costs and decrease in benefits, the ERR would still be
satisfactory at 17%. Details of the calculations are in Annex 5-3 and
in the project file.
Risks
5.12      The project does not involve significant technical risks since
it has been designed according to well-known engineering principles and
would be implemented by competent contractors, and supervised by experi-
enced consultants working with a Port Authority that has administered
the first Port Project very satisfactorily.
C. The Aviation Component
5.13      No ERR has been calculated for the proposed aviation compo-
nent. Its main economic objectives are the development of technically
and financially sound institutions for administering sub-sector activi-
ties and assuring safe operation of essential air traffic services for
the country. The proposed investments in basic navigational aids and
safety equipment, and the modest rehabilitative civil works complement
investments by bilateral donors, and are the minimum required to reduce
the probability of serious air disaster -- which could cost not only
many lives, but a much larger sum than the investments proposed to the
Government -- to acceptable levels as normal in most countries.
The Institution Strengthening Component
5.14      This  component  provides  the  financing for rebuilding  the
overall administration and efficient operation of the transport sector
from the poor foundations left by many years of mismanagement and
neglect. Through technical assistance in the short-term, but especially
through studies and training of Guinean personnel, this contribution to
sector development is essential, even if benefits are not quantifiable.
VI. FINANCIAL EVALUATION OF PAC
A. Recent Financial Performance and Current Financial Position
6.01      The revenue and cash flow objectives set for the implementa-
tion period of the First Port Project were generally achieved, due to
larger than expected traffic volumes, adequate tariff revisions and low
working expenses, including very low personnel costs.  On the whole,
PAC's net income after taxes has averaged US$1.5 m on annual revenues
averaging US$14.6 m over the 1983-86 period.  Cumulated internal cash
generation over the period reached US$18.9 m, allowing PAC to finance
the local cost of the first Port Project, build its working capital at
the required levels and, after deduction of financial charges during
construction and provisions for dredging, accumulate a net cash surplus
of about US$7.7 million.
6.02      PAC's 1985 balance sheet is shown in Annex 6-1 before and
after revaluation, with an estimate of the December 1986 balance sheet.



- 30 -
PAC's balance sheet was fully revaluated by its Auditors; their work
included a detailed evaluation of fixed assets and was a first of its
kind for a Public Enterprise in Guinea. The results of this revaluation
have been agreed with the Ministry of Finance.  Confirmation of this
agreement will be a cor"Jnon of effectiveness.
6.03      From 1983 to 1985, PAC's net accounts receivable increased
substantially to FG 2,974 million by December 1985, or more than nine
months' revenues. These include arrears of local clients, mostly public
enterprises and the administration, as well as of foreign port users.
Several corrective measures were taken in 1986. PAC appointed Hamburg
Port Consulting to completely overhaul foreign client accounts and
collect arrears prior to 1986. In addition, PAC introduced new payment
procedures including advance payments and deposits by shippers, and
started applying stringent measures including detaining delinquent
ships. As a result, actual collection (including arrears) during 1986
on foreign clients reached VS$8.4 m against total billings of US$9.2 m.
Total gross receivables thereby increased only by 16% to GF 5,121 mil-
lion by December 1986, while total revenues increased by 36%. During
the first quarter of 1987, actual collections have exceeded billings by
2%.
6.04      PAC's current assets as of December 1986 include large amounts
deposited and frozen in the liquidated state banks, of which US$8.6 m in
foreign currency. The unavailability of these sums has so far made it
impossible for PAC to have long overdue maintenance dredging carried out
by a foreign contractor. Also, PAC has not yet paid to the Treasury the
interest during construction on the loans for the first Port Project and
some taxes, which constitute the bulk of its current liabilities.
B. Tariffs
6.05      Under the First Port Project, a new tariff structure was put
into effect in 1984, which essentially includes port dues on ships and
stevedoring charges payable in dollars, and wharf-handling and storage
charges for cargo, payable in local currency (for details see An-
nex 6-2). Regular tariff increases were implemented over 1984-85; and
following the 1986 devaluation, an intermediate revision of tariffs
payable in local currency was introduced, which compensated partly for
the effect  of  the devaluation.   Further adjustments of  the tariff
structure are required in 1987, mainly to reflect the transfer of PAC's
stevedoring, shorehandling and warehousing activities to private sector
operators, thereby transforming PAC into an organization dealing essen-
tially with  infrastructure.   A new  tariff  structure prepared by a
consultant was reviewed with the Association before negotiations; its
implementation will be a condition of effectiveness.  To achieve the
financial objectives specified below, regular subsequent tariff increas-
es will be required. The tariff structure will be reviewed again with
the Association before the opening of the new container terminal.
C. Financial Objectives
6.06      PAC's stated objective is to be a fully autonomous, commer-
cially-oriented  and  profitable  public  enterprise.   Its  statutes,
adapted in line with the privatization process, specify that operating



- 31 -
revenues should cover all operating expenses, including adequate mainte-
nance expenditures, interest charges on debt, provision for losses and
renewals, and a minimum return on revaluated net fixed assets.  The
authorities have consistently accepted tariff increases requested by PAC
to achieve stated objectives, but PAC's management has tended to make
inadequate provision for losses. Proper estimation of these and setting
of adequate salaries and incentives for personnel will need special
attention in the annual budget preparation process. As under the first
Port Project, agreement was reached between the Government and IDA that,
during the project implementation period, PAC will submit annual draft
budgets for comment by the Association.
6.07      Progress toward financial autonomy for PAC has been less than
satisfactory with regard to control by the Government of PAC's opera-
tional expenditures and Bank accounts.  At present, all expenditures
above FG 200,000 are submitted to a priori Government control, making
the timely execution of maintenance very difficult. Also, in addition
to the regulation whereby PAC's cash surplus was to be deposited in
non-interest-bearing accounts in the Central Bank, the Government had
requested that payments by PAC's clients be effected directly into
Central Bank accounts, which makes monitoring of client accounts ex-
tremely cumbersome.   During negotiations,  the Government  e E eed to:
(a) limit a priori control to expenditures above US$250.0(0 equivalent;
(b) allow PAC to maintain its accounts in commercial banks; and (c) let
PAC keep its surpluses in interest-bearing accounts. Requisite admin-
istrative measures confirming these arrangements will be a condition of
effectiveness.
6.08      PAC's financial management needs to be reinforced.  The recent
recruitment of a high-level financial expatriate advisor is already a
major step towards this reinforcement. With his help, PAC will prepare
a detailed program covering the strengthening of the accounting system,
the setting up of fail-safe billing procedures based on dependable
information using a new computer system; the introduction of proper
internal controls; and the corresponding training of key personnel
abroad. Agreement of this program by the Association will be a condi-
tion of effectiveness. Its major immediate objective will be to improve
collection of receivables, the levels of which should not exceed three
months of revenues. Other agreements under the ongoing Port Project on
financial targets and covenants, i.e., working and operating ratios,
current ratio, reevaluation of assets and annual audits, and agreement
of the Association on PAC's investment expenditures during project
implementation, were reconducted under the new project (Annex 6-1). In
addition, it was agreed that PAC should maintain a rate of return of at
least 6% on average revaluated net fixed assets in use.
6.09      About GF 3,719 m (US$9.3 m) of PAC's cash, mostly in foreign
exchange, were frozen in the liquidated state banks, and similarly about
GF 733 m was due to PAC by local clients, mostly state enterprises and
administration  departments.   In  addition,  PAC  should  also  collect
through the State about US$3.1 m of arrears from some foreign shipping
lines, that have to be negotiated under bilateral arrangements. Con-
versely, PAC owes to the State GF 2,648 m in interest during construc-
tion on the loans for the First Port Project and about GF 511 m in tax
arrears. PAC prepared a plan for compensation of all reciprocal debts,
which was finalized and agreed upon with the Ministry of Finance before



- 32 -
negotiations. Agreement was confirmed at negotiations. This plan also
includes:  (a) compensation for PAC's foregone revenues of GF 174 m for
the period of 1986 and 1987 during which the mining companies refused to
pay announced tariff increases; and (b) GF 557 m in foregone interest,
when PAC's surplus was frozen in non-interest-bearing accounts.   The
balance of GF 3,375 m in favor of PAC will be used to prepay 21% (equiv-
alent to US$8.4 m) of the outstanding loans for the First Project in
1987, thereby reducing future debt service to acceptable levels and
improving PAC's debt-equity ratio. Completion of all legal documenta-
tion for implementation of this plan will be a condition of effective-
ness.
6.10      In addition to the debt compensation, agreement was reached
with the Government at negotiations to:  (a) consolidate as equity the
former liability labelled "depreciation to be paid to the State" by PAC
under the old accounting system; and (b) allow PAC to carry forward
cumulated losses before paying income taxes.
D. Forecast Financial Performance
6.11      The  financial  forecasts  for  the  1987/95 period have been
developed taking into account that the new tariff structure would be put
into effect by May 1987 and that PAC has relinquished all stevedoring
activities to private sector companies by April 1987. The forecasts, in
current prices, are based on conservative traffic assumptions, stepped
tariff increases and some increases of expenditures in local currency,
the levels of which had not yet been fully adjusted following monetary
reform.   Fixed asset values and the depreciation schedule used are
derived from the revaluation for the closing balance sheet as of Decem-
ber 31, 1985. Major assumptions and considerations used in the forecast
as well as the forecast income account are detailed in Annex 6-3 and in
the Project File. PAC's operating income would be positive all through
project  implementation  and  thereafter.   Under  the  assumption  that
interest on the new loans for the project are charged to the income
statement the same year, net income would become negative in 1989
without tariff increase.   To cover the major  increase  in financial
charges between 1988 and 1992 and loan repavments thereafter, a new
increase in tariffs will thus be needed starting at the latest in 1990.
With total tariff increases of 34% in current terms between 1989 and
1993 (with 5% p.a. in 1989 and 1990, 10% in 1991, and 5% in 1992 and
1993), PAC's income after taxes would be slightly positive in 1989-1991,
and show substantial net profits from 1993 onward, when shipping would
start to fully benefit from the new port facilities. The rate of return
on net fixed assets in use would reach 9% until 1991, and exceed 10%
starting in 1992.
6.12      The forecast statement of source and application of funds and
the forecast balance sheet are also shown in Annex 6-3.  During the
1987-92 period, PAC would cover with its initial cash surplus and cash
flow, some US$11.4 m for the local cost portion of the new project,
maintenance dredging and minimum increases in working capital needs as
well as about US$59.1 m in interest during construction and debt service
on the borrowings for the First Port Project. The substantial addition-
al cash outflow occurring when debt service on the new borrowings would
cumulate with the debt service on the old borrowings, would be covered
by the tariff increases over 1989-92.  In the peak year, 1992, 68% of



- 33 -
PAC's cash expenses would be interest paid to the Government cn the
credits for the first and second port projects re-lent to PAC at commer-
cial  rates.   Even  during  the  two  years  of maximum  cash  outflows
(1989/90), PAC would still generate a small cash surplus. Debt service
coverage ratio would remain a satisfactory 1.3 during project implemen-
tation, increasing to 1.4 by 1995.  PAC's fixed assets would increase
substantially during implementation of the new project with the new
investments being financed mainly by the new loans.  Long term liabili-
ties would thereby increase substantially until 1991. Thereafter, PAC's
debt equity ratio would gradually improve as repayments of principal on
the new loans add to the repayments on the earlier loans.  Agreements
were obtained at negotiations on a debt limitation covenant, excluding
any additional long-term debt during project implementation and featur-
ing a minimum debt-service coverage ratio of 1.25 thereafter.
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.01      During negotiations, agreements were reached or confirmed on
the following:
With SETP:
(a)  study. of funding mechanism for road maintenance to be submit-
ted to IDA by November 15, 1987 and course of action to be
agreed by January 1, 1988 (para 3.02);
(b) annual review of infrastructure maintenance budgets (pa-
ra 3.04);
(c) agreement on transformation of OPR into a private contractor
(para 3.05); and
(d) terms of reference for Project Management Unit in SETP (pa-
ra 4.07).
With SET:
(a) annual review of three-year rolling investment and expenditure
programs  for the transport  sector,  including any proposed
investment for railways (para 3.04);
(b)  transformation of TUC into a joint venture company not later
than June 30, 1988 (para 3.06);
(c) preparation of draft legislation on road transport services by
March 1, 1988, for enactment no later than June 30, 1988
(para 3.07);
(d) employment by PAC of an independent contractor for maintenance
of port facilities by January 1, 1988 (para 3.11);
(e) transfer of maintenance contract for navigation aids in
Conakry port from Office Maritime to private contractor
starting January 1, 1988 (para 3.11);



- 34 -
(f) establishment of an autonomous agency for air navigation
control by June 30, 1988 (para 3.12);
(g) submission by ANA of proposal of adjusted landing fees by
March 1, 1988, and submission of annual budgets to IDA (pa-
ra 3.12);
(h) action plan for completing the AIR GUINEE study financed under
the PPF and for implementing its recommendations; implementa-
tion of restructuring plan to start June 30, 1988 at the
latest (para 3.13);
(i) staff testing, proposals of new operational structure for SET
and SETP, and establishment of a manpower development plan
(paras 3.15, 3.16);
(j) establishment of a Monitoring Unit in PAC for coordination of
project implementation before December 1, 1987; and submission
of a training program for PAC personnel by June 30, 1988
(para 4.17);
(k) respective responsibilities of ANA and the national meteorolo-
gical services (MN) for operating and maintaining meteorologi-
cal equipment (para 4.19);
(1) Government equity contribution to ANA and modalities for
on-lending of credit proceeds to ANA (para 4.30);
(m)  financial targets (6.08) and debt limitation covenant for PAC
(para 6.13); and
(n) consolidation as equity of PAC of liability labelled "depreci-
ation to be paid to the State" under the old accounting
system; and possibility for PAC to carry forward losses for
tax purposes (para 6.10).
7.02      Conditions of Effectiveness will be:
(a) implementation of a GF 15 per liter increase in the tax on
petroleum products (para 3.02);
(b) implementation of agreed new tariff structure by PAC (pa-
ra 3.03);
(c) implementation of revised statutes and regulations of PAC,
together with a new organization and salary structure
(para 3.10);
(d) confirmation of the agreement of PAC's revaluated balance
sheet by the Ministry of Finance (para 6.02);
(e) requisite administrative measures confirming limitation of
a priori control of PAC expenditures to contracts exceeding
US$250,000 equivalent, and permission for PAC to maintain
accounts in commercial banks and keep surpluses in interest
bearing accounts (para 6.07);



- 35 -
(f) detailed PAC program for financial management improvements
(para 6.08); and
(g) completion of legal documentation for compensation of recipro-
cal debts between PAC and the State (para 6.09).
7.03      Conditions of Disbursement will be:
(a)  for the road component:  adoption of an adequate 1988 budget
for maintenance of the priority road network (para 3.02);
(b) for the port and aviation components: conclusion of subsidi-
ary loan agreements between the Borrower and PAC and ANA,
respectively (para 4.14);
(c) for the port component: effectiveness of the AfDB loan and of
the KfW creo-t (para 4.29); and
(d) after January 1, 1988, implementation of a further increase in
fuel taxes (para. 3.02).
7.04      Subject to the above terms, the proposed project is suitable
for a Credit to the Government of the Republic of Guinea in the amount
of US$55 m equivalent, on standard IDA terms.

ANNEX 2-2

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Roads and Road Transport
1.        The situation in the vital road sector is critical.  The state
of maintenance on the 4,200 km priority network which carries over 80%
of all traffic Is extremely poor; of 28 ferries for large river cross-
ings,  only two are operational,  and there are at least 2,500 m of
temporary bridges that need to be replaced.  Currently, the Government
has two major road projects under implementation, both initiated under
the previous regime:   the paving of a 126 km section of the 284 km
Gueckedou-N'zer6kor6  road  in  the  eastern  Forest  Region  and  the
IDA-financed Third Highway Project. The former, with an investment of
US$80.4 m equivalent co-financed in 1983 by four Islamic development
agencies,  is expected  to be  completed  in 1988.   The  Third Highway
Project with a total investment cost of US$73.2 m equivalent is
co-financed by IDA (US$28 m), the African Development Fund (US$14.6 m)
and the Arab Bank for Economic Development in Africa (BADEA) (US$9 m).
This project became effective in December 1984.  Its main objective is
to ensure all-weather traffic on a priority network of 4,200 km.  It
includes reconstruction of the countrv's most highly trafficked road
from Conakry to Mamou (255 km), spot reconstruction and resurfacing of
about 300 km of other paved roads, regravelling of 445 km of laterite
and 800 km of earth trunk roads, and a three-and-a-half year program of
routine maintenance of the priority network, which consists mainly of
road sections rehabilitated under IDA's Second Highway Project.   In
addition to these trunk road projects, feeder road construction, reha-
bilitation and maintenance is being carried out in the Kankan-Siguiri
areas of Upper Guinea (450 km, US$3.75 m equivalent) under a French and
FED-financed cotton project, and in the Gueckgdou area (500 km,
US$2.8 m) under an IDA-financed rice project.
2.        Start-up of the Third Highway Project has been difficult.  It
was first stalled in the general slowdown of Government activities
following the change of regime, later by financing problems, when the
project revolving funds were suspended following the closure of the
banking system and monetary reform in early 1986.  Difficulties also
arose from a project organization designed for the politico-economic
reality of the First Republic where the public administration worked
poorly and private enterprise was not admitted. To circumvent political
interference and compensate for the ineffectiveness of the Public Works
Administration, the project created an autonomous Road Project Office
(Office du Projet Routier, OPR). The OPR was to be responsible for all
planning, programming and control of civil works under the project, as
well as for executing the regravelling program and equipment-based
maintenance of the priority road network through a "Works Division." A
"Consultant-Contractor" was to run the Works Division along the lines of
a contracting firm, and a "Supervising Consultant" was to assist in the
other functions.   This institutional set-up has proved unnecessarily
cumbersome in the changed economic climate of the Second Republic, which
encourages  private  buslness  development.   After  redesigning  of  the
project a gradual process of orienting OPR's structure towards that of a
contracting enterprise has begun, while planning, programming and
control functions are to be returned to the Secretariat of State for



- 38 -
ANNEX 2-2
Page 2 of 4
Public Works. Agreement to the transformation of OPR into a private
contractor was confirmed during negotiation of the proposed project.
3.        Road works included in the Third Highway Project provided no
more than the minimum infrastructure needs of an autarkic economy whose
agricultural sector had been stagnating at self-consumption levels for
many years. They are therefore clearly insufficient to support develop-
ment of a productive market-oriented agriculture sector in a newly-lib-
eralized economy where traffic volumes will soon exceed those forecast
in  1982.   In addition,  many road  sections  that needed only simple
resurfacing when evaluated in 1981 for inclusion under the project now
require substantial reconstruction at significantly higher costs.
Consequently, the Third Highway Project needs to be complemented and
extended, as a matter of urgency, into a priority road rehabilitation
program of the country'8 trunk road network.   The proposed project
includes rehabilitation of those sections of the existing paved road
network not covered under Highways III.  Other priority works will be
carried out under the Road Rehabilitation program to be appraised in
early FY88.
4.        The road transport industry has undergone significant changes
since the advent of the Second Republic.  The most important is the
abolition of two state companies' monopoly of long-distance freight
transport  (COTRA -- dissolved; ENTRAT -- being liquidated).   Freight
traffic is now being handled mostly by private operators; several of
them have acquired truck fleets of 10-20 vehicles, but most are own-
er-operators of one or two vehicles. Access to the industry is free.
Operators from neighboring countries (Senegal, Sierra Leone) have
started to cater to the Guinean market at peak times when demand exceeds
available supply. Road passenger transport has always been handled by
small private transporters and continues to be so. Some 7,000 vehicles
are estimated to be licensed for public transport with over two-thirds
operating in the capital.  There is also still considerable unlicensed
transport for hire, frequently provided by Government employees using
the  administration's  vehicles.   A  newly  established  joint-venture
company (SOGETRAG) started to provide efficient bus services in Conakry
in early 1986, while the surviving state company, TUC, operates poorly
and irregularly:   it is expected to be restructured with a Moroccan
private partner.  Regarding inter-urban passenger transport services,
one private bus company (GUINEE EXPRESS) provides regular services with
some twenty 58-seater buses; but parallel ixregular services have
sometimes been provided by the state-owned "GARAGE DU GOUVERNEMENT"
which charged lower tariffs, since it does not amortize its equipment
provided by the state.   During negotiations,  agreement was reached
between the Government and IDA, on the limitation of the commercial
activity of GARAGE DU GOUVERNEMENT and on a timetable for the restruc-
turing of TUC.
5.        The small private transporters are grouped in syndicates that
organize terminals and allocate and distribute scarce fuel and spare
parts; the large transport companies operate independently, mostly for
regular large clients.  Facilities for vehicle maintenance and repair
are few and those that exist are poorly equipped.  Transport tariffs,
except for urban bus services, are no longer set by SET, but determined
by the operators according to what the market will bear.  When trans-
porters raised tariffs fourfold following a fourfold Increase in the



- 39 -
ANNEX 2-2
Page 3 of 4
price of petrol and a tenfold increase in the price of spares, demand
dropped drastically and tariff increases soon stabilized at about double
the previous levels.   Rates reflect costs fairly accurately and are
higher in rural areas where road conditions are poorest and fuel and
spares supplies most costly.
6.        It  is still difficult  to determine  the  size of Guinea's
overaged and extremely heterogeneous vehicle fleet. The Vehicle Regis-
tration Office, previously in SETP, but recently attached to SET where
it more correctly belongs, keeps track of new registrations only,
without dropping scrapped vehicles from its rolls. The proposed project
will provide technical assistance for the reorganization of the Vehicle
Licensing and Registration Office; it will introduce legislation on axle
load control and begin enforcement of basic technical safety regula-
tions.
7.        In  1982,  the  operational  vehicle  fleet was  estimated  at
21,000. With imports almost doubling to over 7,000 vehicles p.a. (many
of them second-hand) in 1985 and 1986, the fleet is currently estimated
at about 27,000 vehicles, (less than a fifth of that in C8te d'lvoire)
of which about 19,000 passenger cars, 3,000 station wagons, 4,000 trucks
and 1,000 buses. This is adequate to meet present transport demand, but
as economic activity picks up, a considerable increase in transport
capacity will be required. However, major new investment in vehicles by
private Guinean operators can be expected only after normal operating
conditions have been re-established on the country's main road network.

Page 1 of 6
REPUBLIC OF GUINEA
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Fuel Supply, Distribution and Pricing
1.        Fuel supply and distribution have been serious impediments to
normal  development  of  the  road  transport  sector.   Under  the First
Republic, they were the responsibility of two state monopolies:  ONAH
for non-mining related imports and distribution, and HYDROVENTES for
retailing, through a network of 168 service stations.  In 1985, ONAH
absorbed HYDROVENTES and is currently responsible for both fuel supply
and retail distribution, while negotiations with foreign partners to
form a joint venture successor company are underway. For a number of
years, another joint-venture company, (with TEXACO) SOGUILUB, has been
operating a lubricating oil receiving, storage, bleiding and packaging
facility in Conakry, and TEXACO owns the Conakry storage terminal
(11 tanks for 19,000 m9 of gasoline, 15,000 m9 of gas-oil and 3,000 mg
of jet fuel) and operates that of FRIGUIA (19,000 mi).
2.        Between 1972 and 1984, the volume of Guinea's total non-mining
related imports rose from 113,500 to 184,000 ml or by 62% (about 5%
p.a.) overall, but the corresponding increase in cost was from US$3.4 m
to US$41.3 m equivalent or some 1200% (Table 1).  In addition to the
imports of ONAH, Guinea's mining companies imported a total of 255,000
m3 of fuel in 1984, making the mining sector the main consumer of
petroleum products (58% of total country imports). Transport is next in
importance, accounting for 30%. Households and electricity generation
by SNE account for just under 10% of consumption, while that of the
remaining sectors is negligible (Table 2).
3.        Foreign  exchange  shortages  and  the  absence  of  long-term
contracts at times have resulted in erratic and insufficient fuel
supply; poor management and neglect of maintenance of the tanker truck
fleet and of storage and station facilities have led to the almost
complete breakdown of the fuel distribution network.   ONAR currently
disposes of a fleet of 161 vehicles with a total capacity of 1,665 min,
of which 128 (1,381 m9) were in service in 1985. Half of this fleet was
provided to ONAR in 1984/85, while the other half are more than eight
years old and will need to be replaced.  Of the 168 service stations
taken over from HYDROVENTES,  about one  third are  in Conakry.   The
majority, especially outside the capital, are inoperable for lack of
pumps and other spares. ONAH's supply to the provinces has practically
dried up:  in 1984, Conakry, with 12% of the population, accounted for
85% of the country's total fuel consumption -- 197 liters p/cap. com-
pared to 5 liters p/cap. in the rest of the country (Tables 3 and 4).
In practice, much of the supply to consumers outside the capital is
shipped in drums and jerricans carried by road tankers and other vehi-
cles. ONAH's headquarters in Conakry are continually congested with
queuing tankers and unauthorized personnel filling their containers at
two pumps. This costly practice ties ONAH trucks up for abnormally long
periods and constitutes serious fire hazards both at ONAH headquarters
and for vehicles in transit.
4.        Besides its technical deficiencies, ONAH's financial manage-
ment also presents serious problems. Poor accounting procedures result



- 42 -
ANNEX 2-3
Page 2 of 6
In substantial losses:  in the first trimester of 1986, for instance,
ONAR had not billed one-third of its sales. Of the two-thirds billed,
only 50% had been paid, and monthly amounts outstanding were roughly
equal te Guinea's total monthly civil service wage bill.  Given the
heavy cost of ONAH's operations to the Guinean economy, satisfactory
conclusion of the current negotiations with foreign companies and
establishment of ONAH's successor company are important steps which are
expected to occur in the near future. Once a new structure is in place,
rehabilitation of the fuel distribution network becomes an urgent
priority for re-establishing normal road transport conditions.  Grant
financing of about US$9.5 m equivalent for some storage depots, petrol
stations and tanker trucks is available from Italy, but additional funds
may be required.
5.        Since Guinea's fuel prices until early 1986 were based on an
exchange rate less than one-fourteenth of the free market rate, official
fuel prices were very low compared to both international prices and
those in neighboring countries: at a real',atic exchange rate, retail
prices were less than 50% of CIF Conakry prices and about 15% of retail
prices in Senegal. Even after raising pump prices per liter of fuel to
FG 115 and subsequently to FG 140 following monetary reform, they still
represent only one-half to one-third those in Mali, Senegal, or C8te
d'Ivoire at CFAP 285, 320, or 350, respectively.  It is therefore not
surprising that "losses" of fuel to the parallel market and to neighbor-
ing countries continue to be a major problem. To reduce smuggling and,
at the same time, generate revenues sufficient to finance essential road
maintenance, fuel prices must be adjusted upwards until price differen-
tials to neighboring countries disappear. Further, fuel taxes should be
structured so as to favor essential transport services over personal
vehicle use, and to ensure that their burden falls most heavily on those
income groups with the highest ability to pay.
WAPTR, April 1987

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Railways and Rail Transport
1.        Guinea's three enclave mining railways, constructed
since 1965, handle the bulk transport flows they were designed
to accommodate efficiently: the Sangaredi-Kamsar line trans-
ports currently about eight million tons per year (tpy) of
bauxite, the Kindia-Conakry line carries some three mil-
lion tpy of bauxite and the Fria-Conakry line 600,000 tpy of
alumina.   Management  and upkeep  of  the  railways  are  the
responsibility of the mining companies and seem adequate.
2.        The Conakry-Kankan public railroad was constructed
in the period 1900-1914. Up to the mid-1940s, it carried most
of the coutntry's agricultural exports to the Port of Conakry.
Since independence, the railway has been poorly maintained.
As the orLe meter gauge track deteriorated and rolling stock
fell into disrepair, ONCFG's (Office National du Chemin de Fer
de Guin6e) level of service steadily declined; by 1981, the
railroad carried no more than 10,000 tons of freight and
288,000 passengers, three quarters of whom were suburban
commuters.   Acquisition  of  two new  locomotives  and eight
railcars financed by France briefly led to an increase in long
distance traffic in 1984.  An international donors' meeting
including IDA agreed in May 1985 on a program of minimum
investments and technical assistance to maintain rail opera-
tions at existing levels, for a three-year period, while the
roads paralleling the railways were being rehabilitated.
However, by the end of 1985, ONCFG's lack of progress caused
French technical assistance to be withdrawn and the interim
investment program to be cancelled.  In both 1985 and 1986,
earth slides and washed-out bridges interrupted traffic on the
up-country line for several months. Urban passenger traffic
was halted altogether in 1984, although in late 1986, the
grounded long-distance railcars were briefly put into opera-
tion on the 35-km urban line to alleviate road congestion
during the construction of drainage works along the airport
access road.
3.        ONCFG's financial situation under the First Republic
was dismal, with revenues not even covering operating expens-
es. Railway personnel remained unpaid when the fares collect-
ed were not sufficient to cover salaries. After adjustment of
tariffs to realistic levels following monetary reform, the
previously sizeable demand for passenger transport on the
up-country line fell drastically and long distance passenger
services were suspended for lack of clients in early 1986.



- 48 -
ANNEX 2-4
Page 2 of 4
Railway workshop facilities, which for years have provided
much-needed welding and machining services to private custom-
ers, should be officially privatized, possibly by allowing the
personnel currently enjoying the use of its equipment to
purchase it. It is proposed that rather than focussing on the
politically sensitive issue of closing the railways, the
process of attrition be allowed to continue by limiting new
railway investments to those essential for safety reasons.
This limit on investments was agreed at negotiations.
WAPTR
April 1987

ANNEX 2-5
Page 1 of 4
REPUBLIC OF GUINEA
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Conakry Port Facilities
Location and Meteorology
1.        Conakry port (Map IBRD 20075) is located on the west side of
Tombo island.  The weather is hot and humid with a dry season from
December to April and a rainy season (average 4,175 mm per year) from
May to November. High water spring tide reaches 3.8 m above the zero
level, which is the lowest  attained water level.   Tombo island is
connected to the Camayenne Peninsula on the mainland by two dikes
providing rail and road connections. The port is naturally protected
from the west by Kassa island and artificially protected from the north
by  three unconnected breakwaters:   the Prudente breakwater,  1,700 m
long, the North breakwater, 1,000 m long, and the North dike (Epi Nord)
1,000 m long. Port configuration is poor: the two ore-handling facili-
ties (two bauxite berths, 300 m; and two alumina berths, 350 m) that
also serve for unloading fuel and clinker are located at opposite ends
of the port.  Between them are four general cargo berths of 640 m in
all, and a shallow draft basin for fishing boats and passenger craft
serving the islands.
Access to the Port
2.        The leading channel, which runs from south to north, is 5 km
long and 150 m wide. Due to deferred maintenance dredging, the channel
had a depth of only about 7.4 m in early 1987. This will be dredged to
8.5 m under a contract about to be awarded. The channel is marked on
the west side by red light buoys and on the east side by green light
buoys; most of the buoys are not operating and some are incorrectly
located. Conakry Port is connected to the interior by two roads, and by
three railway lines: the alumina line 1.0 m gauge connecting the port
with Fria; the bauxite line 1.435 m gauge connecting the port with
Kindia and the almost defunct public railway line to Kankan.
3.        The following table gives information on the berthing facil-
ities (length, depth), and their utilization and occupancy rate over the
past three years. It shows that the rate of occupancy of all berths has
been steadily increasing resulting in the increase of average ship-wait-
ing time from 17.5 hours per ship in 1983 to 27.2 hours per ship in 1984
and 36.0 hours per ship in 1985. Ship-waiting time during April and May
of each year is about 25% higher than the above averages.

Open Storage Areas
4.         The following open storage areas were paved under the Conakry
Port Project:
-  29,000 m2 for containers
-   3,000 m2 for imported vehicles
-  65,000 m2 for general cargo
-  15,000 m2 on quay aprons
5.         The  proposed  project will  finance  paving  of  an  additional
20,000 m2 within  the  existing  port  and  of  45,000  m2  for  stacking
containers on the proposed container terminal.
Transit Sheds
6.         Three transit sheds were rehabilitated under the Conakry Port
Project (MC1, MC2 and MC3) totalling 9,000 m2.  Several additional small
sheds are located south of the eastern basin, the majority in poor
condition.   These are mainly used by  the Marine,  SNG,  SONIGLACE and
SOGUIPECHE.   The  proposed  project  includes  the  construction  of  a
1,625 m2 shed at the new container terminal.



- 53 -
ANNEX 2-5
Page 3 of 4
Floating Equipment
7.        All floating equipment owned by PAC were purchased under the
Conakry Port Project. These include:
-  2 tug boats 26.5 m long, each equipped with two 945 hp
engines providing a speed of 10 knots and a pull of 27
tons.
-  one pilot boat 14.5 m long with a speed of 9 knots.
-  one mooring boat 10.15 m long with a speed of 9 knots.
The old tug boat "Faranah", constructed in 1954, is in poor condition
and will soon need to be scrapped.
Cargo Handling Equipment
8.        PAC owns the following equipment:
-  one heavy lifting crane 160 ton capacity (1985).
-  two cranes 32 ton (1978) and 20 ton (1984) capacity.
-  two cranes 18 ton capacity each (1978).
-  20 forklifts:  two 28 ton capacity each (1984); two 14
capacity each (1986); six 5 ton capacity each (1981); eight
4 ton capacity each (1984); and two 2.5 ton capacity each
(1984).
-  five tractors 20 ton capacity each (1984) and one 10 ton
capacity (1980).
-  16 trailers 40 ton capacity each, four purchased in 1978
and 12 in 1986.
Most of this will be sold to private operators when cargo handling is
transferred to them.
Recent Developments
9.        In 1982, facilities of the general cargo port had deteriorated
to a point that they were barely able to handle the stagnating volume
(about 300,000 tpy) of non-mining related imports into the country.
Rundown facilities and equipment, and poor management of operations bv
four different Government-owned agencies, resulted in high berth occu-
pancy for general cargo (80%), long ship turnaround time (seven days on
average), low productivity (less than 200 tons per ship/day of general
cargo unloaded), poor security and high pilferage losses. Consequently,
freight rates to Conakry were substantially higher than those for
neighboring ports, and because of the high costs of waiting and cargo
losses, conference lines had practically abandoned service of Guinea to
trampers.
10.       Based on a feasibility study for the rehabilitation of port
facilities and a master plan for Conakry Port development, the Conakry
Port Project was undertaken with a total cost of US$38 m equivalent,
co-financed by an IDA Credit (1382-GUI) of US$13 m equivalent, an



- 54 -
ANNEX 2-5
Page 4 of 4
African Development Bank loan of US$14 m equivalent, and by the Federal
Republic of Germany: KfW, US$6 m equivalent; and GTZ, US$2.7 m equiva-
lent.  The project was approved by the Association in June 1983 and
became effective in December of that year.  Its main objectives were
(a) establishment of an autonomous and financially viable Port Authority
combining the attributes of the four entities previously involved in
port operations; (b) rehabilitation of quays and aprons of the main
commercial berths of the port, storage, rail, road, drainage, workshop
and office facilities; (c) renewal of all floating craft, cargo handling
equipment and vehicles; and (d) technical assistance to assist the new
authority's management in establishing sound operating and financial
procedures.  Implementation of the project has proceeded according to
schedule and project works are expected to be completed in early 1987
with funds fully disbursed eight months ahead of schedule.  Physical
project objectives have been fully realized and institutional objectives
substantially so.
11.       Institutionally,  the newly created (1982) Port Autonome de
Conakry (PAC), with technical assibtance from Hamburg Port Consultants
Ifinanced by GTZ, has evolved into a credible port manager with substan-
tial, though not full financial autonomy as foreseen by its statutes.
PAC took over from its predecessor organizations the responsibilitv for
(a) general port administration; (b) the shiphandling, stevedoring,
warehousing previously handled by ENTRAT; and (c) technical services
such as pilotage, towage and allocation of ships to berths, storage,
civil works, maintenance of infrastructure, maintenance of electricity,
water  and  sewage  systems,  pharmacy,  and  fire  prevention.   Transit
functions have continued to be handled by ENTRAT, and consignment by the
SOCIETE NAVALE  GUINEENNE  (SNG) --   both  state monopolies.   At  the
constitution of PAC, an excessive workforce of 1,500 was reduced to
about 1,100; this has been further reduced to about 730 at end 1986.
Operational productivity was slow to increase while rehabilitation,
demolition and resurfacing works were being carried out, but it is now
improving substantially. At the end of 1985, general cargo unloaded per
ship/day averaged 300 t and ship turn-around time was less than two days
for 75% of the vessels. Moreover, equipment availability, at around 20%
when the project started, was about 70% in late 1986.  As a result,
liner traffic has returned to Conakry and freight rates have decreased
in constant 1983 terms.
WAPTR, April 1987

TEU - twenty foot equivalent unit. By mid-1985, this figure was limited to 4 TEU per hour;
the 6 TEU per hour were achieved in end-1985 as a result of private stevedoring companies
using their own container handling equipment.

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Airports and Civil Aviation
1.        Guinea has ten airports:  an international airport at Conakry,
and nine domestic airfields in BokA, Lab6, Sambailo, Siguiri, Kankan,
Faranah, Kissidougou, Macenta and N'z6rekor6. Three of the latter -- in
Faranah, Labg and Kankan -- have paved runways, but they are in such
poor condition that like the laterite airfields, they can accommodate
only short take-off and landing (STOL) aircraft. The existing secondary
airfields provide good coverage of the country and could compensate for
the absence of all-weather road connections to the outlying provinces.
However, they are in a poor state of maintenance and lack basic naviga-
tional  aids  and  security  equipment.   Therefore,  they  are  often
non-operational, especially during the harmattan period, when dust from
the Sahel affects visibility, and during part of the heavy rainy season
when the roads also become impassable. A rehabilitation program of the
highest priority airfields will be prepared and carried out under the
proposed project.
2.        Meteorological services for air navigation are to be provided
by the M6tZorologie Nationale (MN) which is part of the Ministry of
National Resources, Energy and Environment.  MN's attributions cover
meteorological observation and forecast for the whole country, for
aviation as well as for other sectors for which weather is important,
such as agriculture,  forestry,  etc.  MN has  12 synoptic observation
stations located in the major towns all over the country, which transmit
their data to the Centre Meteorologique National In Conakry.   Total
personnel amount to about 250, of which about 180 are college graduates
and technicians.   Purchases of modern  equipment have been financed
almost exclusively through foreign assistance (UNDP, FED and some
bilateral sources), but MN suffers greatly from the lack of funds for
spares, maintenance  and essential supplies.   In addition,  as major
essential telecommunication equipment is not available, weather observa-
tions cannot be transmitted to Conakry in a timely manner.  Weather
bulletins for the whole country are not available and forecasts are not
carried out.  Normal meteorological services are therefore generally
inadequate at secondary airports, and limited, sometimes below interna-
tional standards, at Conakry Airport where MN's Civil Aviation personnel
often can rely only on general regional observation and forecasts
received from abroad. The inadequacy of meteorological services for air
navigation is especially costly and dangerous for domestic flights.
Dbring the bad seasons, aircraft often have to fly back to their depar-
ture points because they cannot land at their initial destination; or
they land in hazardous circumstances because of the lack of warning on
local weather conditions.
3.        Conakry airport was rehabilitated with French financing in
1983:  a new terminal was constructed (US$13 m), runway improvements
were carried out (US$10 m) and some navigational aids were renewed
(US$2 m). Serious deficiencies have remained, however, with respect to
air traffic control, meteorological services, navigation aids, and
runway security, as well as with overall airport administration.  To
remedy the most urgent problems, some additional financing was provided
by CCCE in 1986 (US$4 m equivalent) for rehabilitation of the control



- 61 -                      ANNEX 2-7
Page 2 of 6
tower and fencing of part of the runway. But passenger security remains
poor while essential navigational aids (ILS) are in dangerously rundown
condition, runway lighting functions only intermittently and fire
fighting equipment is shared with the city of Conakry, where it remains
most of the time.  In late 1986, airline overflights of Conakry due to
unacceptable ground conditions have become routine and the prospect of a
serious air disaster is haunting officials.
4.        While some of the most pressing technical problems at Conakry
airport will have to be addressed immediately with either local or
bilateral financing, Government is aware that "management by crisis"
will persist and development of the sub-sector will be hamstrung, unless
essential  institutional  changes  are  introduced.   A  first  important
initiative has been taken regarding management of the international
airport. In February 1986, a Memorandum of Understanding was signed
between the Governments of Guinea and France for the establishment of a
Joint-venture company which would take over responsibility for the
operation and maintenance of the new airport facilities and of certain
services from the Directorate General of Civil Aviation (DGAC) and AIR
GUINEE, the national carrier previously responsible for ground services
to foreign airlines. The new SOCIETE DE GESTION DE L'AEROPORT DE CONAK-
RY -- SOGEAC, with Aeroport de Bordeaux as foreign shareholder, and
Afroport de Paris (ADP) as techT.ical partner -- will collect landing,
passenger and handling fees and is expected to be financially autonomous
and self-supporting. Final agreement on its statutes was reached at the
end of 1986 and signature of various agreements and of the technical
assistance contract with ADP is expected during the second quarter of
1987.
5.        The proposed arrangements with SOGEAC still left DGAC in SET
responsible for air traffic control and operation and maintenance of
navigational aids in Conakry, as well as for operation of secondary
airfields throughout the country.  In matters of regional flight con-
trol, Guinea is also member of Flight Information Region (FIR) Roberts,
a multinational agency created with Liberia and Sierra Leone, to provide
air control services at medium and high altitudes, similar to other FIRs
throughout the world.  FIR Roberts has a permanent organization with
about 50 persons based in Liberia, and is funded essentially through the
overflight fees collected from airlines, supplemented by limited Govern-
ment contributions. The major role in air navigation control, such as
domestic control, approach and airport control, including maintenance
and operation of nav-aids and telecommunications, is played by the DGAC.
As a public sector agency, DGAC has consistently faced both budgetary
and technical problems in carrying out its mandate. A long-term techni-
cal assistance input from ICAO with UNDP financing has produced a core
of well-trained technicians and engineers, but in the absence of an
institutional framework allowing them to operate efficiently and be
properly remunerated for their technical skills, outputs have been
disappointing. Maintenance and renewal of essential ground equipment
and other facilities have been insufficient. For example, the 1986 DGAC
budget provided only about tTS$120,000 in maintenance expenditures.
6.        To achieve real improvements in the provision of air naviga-
tion services, it is desirable that DGAC's role be one of planning and
supervision of sub-sector activities within SET, while the delivery of
highly technical services should be ensured by an autonomous agency,



- 62 -                      ANNEX 2-7
Page 3 of 6
outside the civil service, and organized along the lines of local
representations established in most of the francophone countries in the
region by Agence pour la S4curitA de la Navigation A6rienne en Afrique
et A Madagascar (ASECNA), a regional agency.  Agreement to the estab-
lishment of such an agency was confirmed with Government at npgotia-
tions, since IDA involvement in the rehabilitation of secondary air-
fields (runways, facilities, equipment) could be justified only if
satisfactory institutional arrangements for their correct operation and
maintenance were also made.   The proposed project,  therefore,  will
include support for both the institutional and physical rehabilitation
of the civil aviation sub-sector (paras 3.10 ff.).
7.        International air Passenger traffic has increased significant-
ly from its modest level of around 10,000 passengers p.a. in the early
eighties. The number of airlines serving Conakry has increased and now
includes AIR AFRIQUE, UTA, SABENA, KLM, AEROFLOT, ROYAL AIR MAROC,
NIGERIAN AIRWAYS,  AIR ZAIRE,  GHANA AIRWAYS  and AIR IVOIRE.   Total
aircraft movements reached 3,662 in 1985.  Passenger volume increased
from 118,000 in 1983 to 191,000 in 1985. By contrast, local air passen-
ger traffic between Conakry and regional centers, which had attained
90,000 passengers p.a. in the early eighties (including about 40%
Government officials flying free), had dropped sharply to some
10,000 passengers in 1984 and 28,000 in 1985.  International freight
traffic varies between 3,000 and 4,000 tpy.
8.        The decline in local air traffic is due to the poor state of
airport infrastructure and to the highly precarious state of the nation-
al carrier, AIR GUINEE. Created as a public enterprise in 1960, AIR
GUINEE was originally equipped with Russian aircraft for local services.
In 1980, it replaced five of its seven turboprop aircraft by three jet
aircraft (one B707, one B727 (secondhand) and one B737 (purchased with
EXIM Bank credit and commercial credits of US$7.4 m) and re-started
regional and international flights. However, revenues from ticket sales
(at official rates of the highly overvalued sylis for some, and at
parallel market rates to the rest) were consistently insufficient to
cover costs, and the company, grossly over-staffed with more than
450 employees, relied on state subsidies to stay operational.
9.        Discussions with Canada for assistance to domestic aviation
and reform of AIR GUINEE started in 1983. They were pursued under the
Second Republic and resulted in a grant to Guinea in 1984 for acquisi-
tion of a DASH 7 airplane and for several years of technical assistance
in its operation, provided through a training arrangement with QUEBEC
AIR.  However, the problem of full-scale restructuring of AIR GUINEF
along commercial lines was not addressed. Faced with mounting losses in
1985, Government requested a study of AIR GUINEE's operations and
financial situation, with a view to reorganizing the company.  This
study, undertaken in December 1985 with ICAO-financing, recommended the
restructuring of AIR GUINEE into a new company limited to domestic air
services in the first years of operatlon, to be extended to a regional
network as and when operational and market conditions were suitable. It
was estimated that such an operation could be financially self-support-
ing, with the new airline collecting fees from pooling arrangements with
foreign airlines which would exploit most international traffic rights
with their own more efficient aircraft.



- 63 -
ANNEX 2-7
Page 4 of 6
10.       To stem foreign exchange  losses, Government suspended all
international operations of AIR GUINEE in early 1986, and subsequently
sold two (B707, B727) of the three jet planes, since they cannot be used
on domestic flights. These are now being handled by the DASH-7 exclu-
sively, although one ANTONOV-12 belonging to the Ministry of Defense
continues to provide public air transport to N'zdr6kor6 and other
destinations. By end-1986, discussions on restructuring AIR GUINEE had
advanced little beyond an outline "contrat plan" drafted by the company
itself.   According  to  this  document,  the  company  would  achieve
break-even operation, with 430 employees, using the DASH-7 domestically
and the B737 for regional flights to Dakar, Bamako and Abidjan -- a
clearly unrealistic proposal given the competition from a number of
other airlines on the regional routes. Given the onerous debt service
obligations on the B737, its high fixed costs, excessive capacity, and
its inappropriateness for local services, it would seem preferable that
Government divest itself of this plane as well, or at least lease it out
under a long-term contract.
11.       The Government requested a final study of AIR GUTNEE financed
under the PPF for the proposed transport sector project, and to be
completed by July 1987, under terms of reference agreed with IDA. This
study will establish AIR GUINEE's current assets and liabilities,
propose alternatives for restructuring the company or replacing it by a
successor organization able to provide efficient domestic, and possibly
regional, air services, and develop a scheme for dealing with AIR
GUINEE's debt arrears. It will provide a basis for the Government to
call for specific proposals from foreign airlines to provide technical
assistance or, preferably, participate as equity partners in the re-
structuring of AIR GUINEE, and help exploit the international traffic
rights in the most favorable conditions for the country. At negotia-
tions, Government and IDA agreed on a schedule for completing the study
and implementing its recommendations.
WAPTR, April 1987

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Public Works Administration
1.        As is true for practically all ministries, SETP and SET have
inherited large numbers of inadequately qualified staff from their
predecessor organizations.   These  include a large number of local-
ly-trained civil and mechanical engineers and technicians. The latter
have followed a three-year course at the Ecole Nationale des Arts et
M6tiers, and the former have received an additional two years of train-
ing at the Conakry Polytechnical Institute. However, since both schools
lacked training equipment and teachers with good practical experience,
the qualifications of their graduates are highly theoretical and inade-
quate.   The same holds true for economists and accountants.  Little
selection of staff in terms of their qualifications was possible in the
past, since all graduates were automatically entitled to a civil service
position and ministries were simply assigned their quota.
2.        The public works administration is numerically by far the most
important. In early 1986, SETP had a total of 455 employees in Conakry,
and about 2,000 more in the 36 sub-divisions throughout the country; two
autonomous services supervised by SETP (OPR and the Geodetic and Topo-
graphic Service) accounted for another 200 staff (see Table below). A
new organization chart developed by the organization study reflects
Government's sound intention to concentrate in SETP the planning,
programming and control functions that are its main responsibility,
while transferring execution of major road works to the private sector
whenever possible. SETP would maintain the responsibility for routine
road maintenance administration through the sub-divisions. Implementa-
tion of an appropriate organizational set-up will be among the institu-
tional issues to be addressed under the upcoming road rehabilitation
program.

REPUBLIC OF GUINEA
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Transport Sector Administration
1.        As the new Government's policies evolve and are being trans-
lated into reality, streamlining of administrative structures is a high
priority.  In the first half of 1986, a census of all public sector
employees was carried out with IDA financing; on average, this effort
reduced the salary rolls by 8-10% due to elimination of absentee work-
ers. This is to be followed by a program of "testing" for all civil
servants to identify those best qualified to assume the positions
expected to be retained in a reorganized and much leaner administration,
which would then be paying remunerative salaries. To help this restruc-
turing, organizational studies of both SET and SETP were completed under
a Transport Plan Study being carried out as part of the Third Highway
Project, but their recommendations needed to be refined. The approach
to developing the basic operational structure of SET was agreed with
Government during appraisal and confirmed during negotiations,   This
approach will permit the definition of manpower and training needs so
that financing can be provided under the project for a comprehensive
staff development program designed to meet them.
2.        The administration of the transport sector as such is less
staff intensive than that of Public Works. In early 1986, SET employed
some  90 staff  organized  into  10 directorates  and  divisions.   Three
directorates were responsible for policy development in the sub-sectors
and, more substantially, for overseeing the public sector agencies that
have, until recently, monopolized transport sector activities.  These
directorates were (a) the "Direction des Transports Terrestres" which
dealt with four public enterprises employing some 1,890 staff; (b) the
"Direction des Transports Maritimes" which oversaw two public services
employing some 1,670 staff; and (c) the "Direction des Transports
A6riens" which dealt with two public services and one Public Enterprise
(AIR GUINEE  (460) air transport),  employing about  1000 staff.   With
successive privatization of transport sector activities in accordance
with the market-oriented policies of the Second Republic, SET's role in
the management of sector activities will be phased out. It will instead
focus on establishing an appropriate regulatory framework for sound
development of private sector operation and ensuring its application,
and on the hitherto almost totally neglected tasks of transport plan-
ning. The present organization of SET, which differs from that devel-
oped by the organization study, was reviewed during appraisal and a
program for developing job descriptions for an appropriate organiza-
tional structure and for carrying out staff testing over the next months
was agreed upon. This program was confirmed during negotiations.
WAPTR, April 1987
